B003HARV4S|5.0|From the very beginnings of cinema, especially with the turn-of-the-century movies of the great French magician, Georges Melies, film has been the ultimate illusion.  It's the amateur spectator who perceives the movies as a \\"documentation of reality.\\" Orson Welles, for one, knew better, saying \\"Film is a ribbon of dreams.\\"  And as the illusionist tells his agitated admirers out in the streets: \\"Everything you've seen is an illusion, a trick. Go home and don't be upset.\\"<br /><br />Accordingly, the first step toward the full appreciation of film is to see every great movie as a \\"construct\\" of the director, not as a \\"record of reality.\\"  The Chief Inspector (Paul Giamatti) is the ideal spectator.  In his first confrontation with the Illusionist, he is allowed to see through Illusionist's illusion to the cause-effect logic behind the deception (identifying the behind-the-back hand that holds an object).  He, like the spectator, has seen a deception exposed, though something in us simultaneously wishes to believe in the magic.  As he tells the Illusionist (Edward Norton) during their final conversation, \\"I don't know whether you actually bring these people back from the 'other side' or are playing some great trick on us.  But I do know that you're special.\\" Alfred Hitchcock may not have put Gary Grant on the face of Mt. Rushmore, but we nonetheless believe whole-heartedly in the director's sleight of hand even as we appreciate and applaud his inventiveness.<br /><br />But then we reach the final scene of the film and not only witness but participate in an ever-crescendoing epiphany of joy, one which we share with the Chief Inspector, as he (along with the spectator) finally puts the pieces together: he figures out the illusion. It's ironic that many experienced filmgoers can become so savvy about special effects and complain that the \\"blood didn't look real,\\" etc., yet fail in this instance to be in step with let alone ahead of the Inspector.  Yet the film presents us not with \\"real\\" scenery and backgrounds but with painting after painting, fabrication after fabrication. There's humor just in that premise alone and people don't get it: a Western that is set on an island off the coast of Delaware?! That's about as far removed from the Old West as you can get.<br /><br />Even the characters on the island don't make sense, some have Irish accents, others Southern, while others have Northeastern accents. I'll admit, when I first saw it I was a little put off. I laughed, but I just thought it was okay. Then I saw it again, and I noticed a few things that I missed before. From there on the movie just grew on me. I love it now. The humor in it, some of it is intentional, some unintentional, is hilarious. But the film probably would not have worked without Alan van Sprang and Kenneth Walsh, who play \\"Nicotine\\" Crockett and Patrick O'Flynn, respectively. Every scene these guys are in is entertaining and it's even better when they share the screen, Mr. Sprang is great as the tough guy and Mr. Walsh is a comedic genius as the wisecracking Irishman looking to kill any of the walking dead.<br /><br />If there is anything wrong with this movie, it's the zombies. They are just not scary at all. For me, DAY OF THE DEAD holds the title for scariest zombies in a Romero film. But don'tlet that stop you from watching this movie. I know you're going to say, \\"But the zombies are the most important thing about a zombie movie,\\" but that's not true. It's the people and their fight for survival that's most important. George Romero knows what he's doing and he's done it again.<br /><br />Side note: there is also a fantastic documentary included with the Ultimate Edition that is almost as long as the movie itself, and it takes the viewer through the whole production process and first screenings of the movie.<br /><br />I will guarantee that SURVIVAL OF THE DEAD develops a cult following in the years to come. The fans will come around to appreciate Crockett, O'Flynn, and the rest of the gang.

I am, however, anxious to read the upcoming sequel. B004VUP8LG|4.0|Finally, a new Morbid Angel record! And with David Vincent! But is it any good? ... well I liked it. Yes:    Omni Potens: Aw man, this track totally has a classic Morbid Angel cheese factor about it. Keyboard orchestrations and a David Vincent choir, it's a sweet intro, reminds me personally of an old Warhammer computer game.    Too Extreme!: Yes, lame title, this album is filled with them. This song definitely warns you right off that this is a much different Morbid Angel album. With techno influences and wail guitar in the background, it's starts out a fun track, but it's \\"pounding rhythm\\" really gets annoying after the first three minutes, and it's a six minute track. Vincent even says \\"Pounding pounding!\\" at one point, yeah, no crap. I usually skip this track. This song is three minutes too long and gets really tired quick, so this song is a miss for me. Great riffs, great chorus, great song!    Blades for Baal: Another rocking track. Vincent's vocals sound particularly guttural on this song. Morbid Angel display here that they can still release some genuinely awesome Death Metal.    I Am Morbid: Another goofy title, but another awesome song. This is probably my favorite on this record, it's got a cool vibe with a crowd chanting \\"Morbid!\\" under this sludgy call-back to Domination and also features a fantastic pre-chorus. This is the song that sold the album for me.    10 More Dead: Well what can I say? Four cool tunes in a row. Like the previous tracks, this one sounds like something previously from their catalog (Heretic to be specific), but with David Vincent helming the vocals, of course. The lyrics are a little laughable here, though, the riffs are very rockin' and features more classic Azagthoth guitar solo work.    Destrutos Vs. the Earth / Attack: Ha! Here's a song I though I'd be scratching my head at, and I wasn't that far off, but it's in a good way. This song is a bit more techno influenced, but it's fun! If's obviously suppose to be a bit tongue-in-cheek... I mean, just look at the subject matter! If you don't take it too seriously as well you may end up getting a kick out of it, too.    Nevermore: Back to a more classic Morbid Angel sounding song, most people have heard this song by now having been played live over the past several years, but it's even better on the album. More, blast beats, more Trey solos, some Morbid Angel tremolo picking riffs, but it's got a different vibe from previous song and doesn't really sound like something from a previous release.    Beauty Meets Beast: It's at this point I started to wonder why people hate this album... this is the seventh song on the album that sounds worthy of being in the Morbid Angel catalog. It's a great Death Metal song like most of the previous songs, and like the previous songs it presents a much different vibe. This one has great lyrics and a glorious bridge/solo section.    Radikult: Yeah, this is the one that even people who like the album tend to hate. This isn't very Death Metal at all, sounds like a mix between Rob Zombie and Marilyn Manson. The lyrics are a bit weird too, and are very un-like Morbid Angel, dealing with cults and radicals (btw, the opening lyrics are \\"killer cult\\" not \\"Kill-a-cop\\", common misheard lyrics) easily some of the weaker lyrics on the record. Despite this, I dig the track. Vincent sings it well, I dig the industrial influences, it's got a pretty sweet solo section, and the outro is quite awesome and atmospheric, very reminiscent of Omni Potens. I don't expect many to agree with me on this one, those who dislike the industrial music song are definitely not gonna like this one, and knowing that it came from Morbid Angel are gonna make them like it even less.    Profundis - Mea Culpa: This outro track I found to be pretty weak. Very electronic and it seems to be all over the place, a bit of a mess if you ask me. There's just nothing really going on for this track and is a quite weak end for the album.    So, final verdict, 9 great/good tracks, 2 misses. I really think people are making this album out to be worse than it actually is, there are alot of great Death Metal tracks on here that are being overshadowed by the experimental songs that most people did not want to hear on a Morbid Angel record. But I think a Morbid Angel fan with open mind may find a lot here to enjoy. I will admit that this wasn't the album I was hoping for as the first under in the second David Vincent-era of Morbid Angel. Although I really like this record, I hope their 'J' album is a more 'full-blooded' Death Metal release.

B000BT97AO|2.0|MirrorMask is a lukewarm offering from the Jim Henson Company that suffers from uneven scripting, bizarre visuals with no sense of intrinsic logic, barely audible dialogue, a jarring score, dark and muddy gels, and an over-reliance on computer graphics. The movie had a great cast and some potential, but it's about as watchable as a computer game. If Jim Henson had been alive to make this, he would never have substituted puppetry--an art that he kept alive for years--with substandard CGI effects.     Lacking in tight plotting, sensical narration and fantastical charm, and far too computerized for its own good, MirrorMask is a somewhat entertaining psychological fantasy story featuring fun characters, a plucky heroine, and interesting--albeit muddy and half baked--scenery. This film has some strong points to recommend it. The subtlety of yesteryear is not one of them.     Rumor has it the Jim Henson company noticed Dark Crystal and Labyrinth DVD's flying off the shelves, and in an effort to milk the cash cow for all it was worth, cooked up a movie LIKE those films instead of a sequel that may or may not have gone wrong. Then they roped Neil Gaiman into writing the script, which he set about doing by watching an hours-long uncut version of Labyrinth (lucky man). The result is a movie about an adolescent girl with family issues who journeys through a surreal fantasy realm of her own invention to save the life of a family member. Sound familiar?     The premise is hardly new, and the queens of darkness and light obviously symbolize aspects of Helena's mother. I saw something very similar to this in a cartoon called \\"Oyayubi Hime\\" a.k.a. Japanese Thumbelina, in which the heroine encounters a good and evil witch in a dream land before returning to the real world and her mother. MirrorMask is not a bad idea by any means, but it's a definite retread of better films that had more complex things to say about growing up.     Consider Labyrinth. Are we to view it as a literal adventure, a dream, or Sarah's way of retreating into her mind to deal with her problems? On the surface it appears that Sarah's adventure actually happened, if it wasn't for the toys scattered throughout her room. They assume lifelike stature and importance inside the labyrinth, particularly the figurine on her desk that resembles the Goblin King, making us question just how real the rest of the story is, and what it would show us of Sarah's subconscious if she had invented it. Is Labyrinth just a simple story about a girl's transition from childhood to adulthood and the learning of responsibility? Is it about her distrust of sexuality? Or could there be a darker subtext buried in the film: victimization by an older man and the search for lost innocence, symbolized by the missing baby? Many interpretations are possible.     By contrast, MirrorMask spoon feeds us every maternal symbol as if we couldn't see it coming. Helena's fantasy world directly reflects the real world, and the symbols she encounters (the light queen in an enchanted sleep is her mother in the hospital) make it much more obvious that it's a dream fueled by her worries over her mother. By focusing exclusively on the mother-daughter issue (which Sarah also had), MirrorMask is too confined, too derivative in terms of plot, and I would say too tame. It's essentially Labyrinth-lite without the kinky weirdness, which renders it more simplistic and far less interesting. Where's Bowie when you need him?

0767817710|5.0|The western is by definition a light genre designed as pure escapist entertainment. At first glance, this is just another typical specimen. Apart from the woman with the gun, everything else in the plot is one cliche after another: gunfight contest with lots corpses, old revenge burning with everlasting fire, relentlessly evil villain who terrorises a city, a brash young fellow under the illusion that he is the fastest on the West, a reverend who is ex-bandit. Yet, despite these grave shortcomings, I would rank this as the finest western after [[ASIN:B0000AUHPG Once Upon a Time in the West]] and [[ASIN:B00005RHGL Tombstone]]. Why?     To begin with, the plot may be as hackneyed as they come, but it does have quite a few highly dramatic situations, and these are made the best of by a simply great cast. Gene Hackman fits perfectly into the role of the sadistic fellow who feeds on people's terror: a great actor who can convey menace very convincingly with but a glance or a word. Sharon Stone is, of course, quite hot but she is a fine actress nonetheless for that; hers is a convincing performance of the 'tough girl' who is not nearly as tough as she looks. Both Russell Crowe and the young Leo di Caprio were more or less completely unknown at the time, but both of them show here a most promising talent as the God-fearing priest-murderer and the incredibly boastful youngster, respectively; well over a decade later, both Russell and Leo have quite fine careers behind their backs, so I guess the fulfilled that promise. The duel between Harrod and Ace Hanlon, the scene in the bar in the beginning, the death of the Kid: there is hardly a scene in the whole movie which is without some merit indeed. And who can forget such a dialogue:     He: You're pretty!   She: You're not.   He: I need a woman!   She: You need a bath.     But the real star in the director Sam Raimi. Thanks to highly imaginative and effective way of directing, almost every scene in the movie is a minor masterpiece of film-making. Unusual visual angles, terrific close shots, quick changes, slow-motion, zoom: you name it: everything is used and put to the best use. Take, for instance, the first appearance of Herrod; this is what I call genius for directing. Or pretty much each of the numerous gunfights; none of them is less than memorable, nor are two of them alike. Also, the surreal yellowish lighting used during more or less the whole movie additionally makes it even more compelling, as if taking place on some other planet, or in a dream perhaps.     No, this is not the greatest western ever made. But it is one of the most unforgettable and one of the most entertaining all the same. It is a fine example how to make a great movie out of perfectly mediocre script - provided, of course, that you have fine cast and great director.

0061726826|2.0|I read Lauren Oliver's Before I Fall and LOVED it so much that I immediately purchased Delirium and started reading.  Unfortunately, it falls flat.  As many other reviewers have mentioned, the book starts out very interesting and engaging.  Lauren Oliver is a great writer and this really shows in the first half of the book.  The two best friends, Hana and Lena, are both three-dimensional, believable and unique and I was interested in seeing where their story led.  Alex, the main male protagonist, is realistic (unlike many male leads in love stories) which may not inspire passionate obsession from a reader, but its refreshing to have a kind, intelligent, realistic male after a trend toward taciturn, moody, divinely gorgeous males I've found in other young adult novels.  However, somehow Peeta Mellark (of Hunger Games) managed to capture my attention and affection even though he is realistically kind, caring, thoughtful, and verbally engaging, and Alex never did.  Anyway, I digress.  Still, the story had three solid main characters and an interesting, if not wholly believable, premise.  And then, it foundered painfully during its second half.  The prose became extremely melodramatic and long-winded (it easily could have been shortened to 300 pages).  And we just spent too much time hearing about the profundity of love, entangled in lengthy descriptions of how glowing and beautiful the world appeared to Lena after she'd discovered love.  Don't get me wrong, love is great, but I think that the melodrama cheapened the love, making it unrealistic and boring.  I just wanted Lena to stop waxing poetic about love and the beauty of the world and get back to the story.    I need to focus or this review will become as long-winded and tedious as this novel.  Ultimately, I think this book suffered from two things: 1) the author became too obsessed with her own ability to write beautiful prose and to make a simile/metaphor out of everything and lost focus on the pacing of the actual story. 2) The common problem of the first book in a trilogy, in which the book feels like an incomplete set-up for the two books that will follow.  I think that the brilliance of the Hunger Games (book one) and all of the Harry Potter books was that each book had its own story arc that was fulfilling to the reader, even though there was still more story to read and unanswered questions to get to in the sequels.  For me, the story arc of Delirum was poorly developed and too many questions were left unanswered. A part of me wants answers to those questions, but not enough to endure the long-winded prose of two more books...I think I'll just end up reading the synopses of Pandemonium and Requiem on wikipedia...which means that this book failed, at least for me.

Everything was very detailed, which helped to really set the scene. The author didn't take liberties or make assumptions, as I've seen in other materials about MM. He makes her very human, and gives her credit where she deserves it, but still is truthful about her downfalls and flaws. And perhaps my favorite part of the book was how he ended it. Spoto doesn't buy into all of the conspiracy theories we've all heard. He carefully explains the events of Marilyn's death and the days following and explains why those theories are not and could not be true. I personally love his progression; the shouting doesn't bother me, I find the lyrics very clever even at their most corny, and I love the fast rapping style. It's impossible for me to review an album like this one-hundred percent objectively, so I must share these thoughts with you beforehand. I'm a fan of the song \\"Bad Meets Evil,\\" but I listened to this album for Eminem. I'm sure I won't be the only person who listens to it and wants to hear more of Royce though.     Eminem tends to destroy other rappers when he collaborates with them. If not, at the very least, he seems to be rapping on a different track (think of \\"No Love\\" with Lil Wayne). On this album, Royce proves to be one of the few rappers who can hold their own against him. These two have a very distinct chemistry that brings out the best in one another. Eminem and Royce Da 5'9' are two brilliant rappers/lyricists at the top of their game who have crafted a jaw-dropping EP that was definitely worth the 12-year-wait. Here's a breakdown of the tracks:    1. Welcome 2 Hell - A brief, atmospheric opener that sets the tone for the album. It's essentially an introduction, but it's a great one that instantly hooked me. A haunting beat with the two rapping over it like their life depends on being better than the other. (5/5)    2. Fast Lane - The first single. Most people have probably heard this track already and they probably agree that it's a great hip-hop track. Accessible, but not overproduced or toned-down. Even the biggest Eminem fans will probably agree that Royce delivers the better verses, although they run neck-and-neck throughout. You'll most likely giggle the most at Eminem's \\"Nicki Minaj\\" line though. (5/5)    3. The Reunion - Many Eminem fans will see this as a sort of return to form. The hook (sung by Eminem) is similar to [[ASIN:B00006690G The Eminem Show]]-era hooks rather than the more high-pitched Recovery-era hooks. The subject matter hearkens back to Relapse a bit, with Em even making fun of that record. It features probably the slowest flow from both Em and Royce than any other song on the album, but will likely be a fan favorite. (5/5)     4. Above the Law - A catchy hook with Eminem and Royce spitting fire on their verses make this a terrific track. Some will be turned off by Eminem's verse as he starts off angry and then spends the last 30 seconds or so full-on shouting. Admittedly, the first time I heard it I was even like \\"Whoa, stop yelling,\\" but his rage-filled verse has grown on me substantially. Royce once again shows off his ability to go neck-and-neck with Em and will be the saving grace of the track for some. (5/5)    5. I'm On Everything - The most \\"wtf?\\" track on the record. Sampling a bit from Mike Epps' stand-up and turning it into the hook (I must admit, a creative idea by producer Mr. Porter), \\"I'm On Everything\\" has Royce and Em rapping about their massive drug intake. It's a song that the two were obviously just having a bit of fun with. While the track has grown on me, neither of their individual verses are particularly good; they sound sloppy, thrown-together, and a bit lazy. It's when the two start switching back-and-forth at breakneck speed that the song actually gets impressive. Also, will Eminem ever grow out of throwing in flatulence sound effects onto his records? It's a song that many will disregard as a waste, but I'm actually (embarrassed to admit that) I'm quite partial to it. (4/5)    6. A Kiss - This song has already gained some attention for a supposed diss directed towards Lady Gaga. Well, it's not a diss; it's Eminem joking about an old rumor and the fact that people are getting offended by it is remarkable. Eminem has been doing this for years. While the line about Lady Gaga is not particularly clever, it's far from a big deal. The track itself has a strange beat and an even stranger hook, but it's become one of my favorite tracks on the album. Eminem tones down the yelling significantly, which will please many and the slower, but rapid flow that Royce and Em have makes this stand-out. It took a few listens to grow on me, but it's one of the most infectious tracks on the record. (5/5)    7. Lighters - This song will be the second single and features pop star Bruno Mars singing the hook. It's a complete departure from the rest of the album's sound and seems to be here for the singular purpose of pushing more units. I'm not a fan of Mars, but I don't have anything against him or his music either. With that said, his hook brings  a softness that is not present anywhere else on the album and this song will likely help this album to sell way more copies than it would have without it. However, it's because of that hook that I haven't been able to greatly appreciate the song. The track boasts the most mature, focused lyrics of the entire album from both Eminem and Royce, but the hook by Mars just takes it into too poppy of a territory for me. If you like/love Mars, then you'll no doubt love this song...And once again, Em has toned down the yelling. (4/5)    8. Take from Me - A song that deals with music piracy (among other things). There's a lot of anger from both rappers and both deliver the goods. Even if you're pro-piracy and roll your eyes at the lyrics, you can't deny the feeling that's present in the track and the overall ability that's displayed on this track. The hook may annoy some (it's annoyingly catchy), but this is a great track. (5/5)    9. Loud Noises - This features rap supergroup Slaughterhouse and despite them all delivering sick verses, it's not one of my favorite tracks. I'm not a fan of the random \\"SLAUGHTERHOUSE!\\" yell that occurs before the beginning of every verse nor do I like it as the album closer. Despite these reservations, I do like it and there's enough talent here to make me look into Slaughterhouse's records. (4/5)    The two tracks included with the deluxe edition are \\"Living Proof\\" and \\"Echo,\\" which leaked onto the internet many months ago and most huge fans have heard. \\"Living Proof\\" is basically the same at the leaked version. Royce kind of takes the lead on this one and it's a very raw, stripped-down, hip-hop song and an easy 5/5. \\"Echo\\" has Eminem and Royce's verses switched and a couple small production changes throughout the song. I'm glad they decided to include these tracks on here and release them officially, as they're both gems that don't deserve to just float around the internet in an un-mastered form.    So that's what you get from this EP from a terrific duo whom I hope will do more in the future. I don't know of another rapper besides Royce that could work so well with Eminem on a record. The two really complement each other nicely and it's nearly impossible to say one is better than the other on any given song. There's no point really, as they establish themselves as equals in both lyrical-ability and flow. Rarely does something that some have waited 12 years for live up to the hype, but Hell: The Sequel does just that. It's a stunning display of raw ability and impassioned expression and should definitely put Royce Da 5'9\\" on the mainstream map where he belongs. There will be those that don't share the same ecstatic view I have of this album, but many others (including those who gave Recovery a lukewarm reception) will see this as a return to form for Eminem. The lyrics range from funny to clever to poetic; the beats from sparse to haunting; the flows from lightning-quick to laid-back. This is an all-around great record.    GRADE: A-

B00003CXSP|2.0|Taken chronologically, Hannibal Lecter started out as a brilliant schemer in Red Dragon, refined his - ahem - tastes in Silence of the Lambs, and if the last installment is any measure, became a traditional slasher in Hannibal.<br /><br />Hannibal (Anthony Hopkins) has always been the more gentlemanly of two evils.  Whether it was The Tooth Fairy in Red Dragon or Buffalo Bill in Silence of the Lambs, Lecter seemed pleasant and refined in comparison to the more brutal serial killers. This is what makes an anti-hero so much fun; he's bad, but he's not as bad as the bad guy.  The even-worse villain in Hannibal is Mason Verger (Gary Oldman), a wealthy child molester whom Hannibal convinced (through judicious application of mind-altering drugs) to cut off his own face.  A crippled, revolting shell of a man, Verger seethes with an unholy vengeance and, thanks to his immense wealth, crafts a far-fetched plan to get revenge.<br /><br />And this is where the problems with Hannibal begin. Although it's clear Verger was bad - and presumably, in the Levels of Evil, we can assume molestation of children is worse than cannibalism of adults - but he simply doesn't have the physical presence of the other serial killers to justify Hannibal's own villainy.  Verger is more disgusting than vile, and the shock of the character wears off after a while. Verger's schemes border on cartoon-character levels with his plans to have Hannibal's face eaten by specially bred wild boars.  Yes, that's right - Hannibal's up against a cripple and his army of killer pigs.<br /><br />The first third of the film is a cat-and-mouse game between Chief Inspector Rinaldo Pazzi (Giancarlo Giannini) and Hannibal in Florence, Italy.  This is the Hannibal we know - crawling inside his victim's heads, using their secret shame as a weapon against them, and giving morally flawed human beings their comeuppance for being so gauche. Pazzi's ending is never in doubt, but the increasingly high stakes between the two men makes for thrilling drama.<br /><br />Then we're back to Clarice Starling (Julianne Moore). You'll recall that Clarice was a woman trying to make it in a man's world in Silence of the Lambs.  Forget all that - here, Starling as embodied by Moore is a delicious peach, to be ogled by the camera as it slow pans not once but twice over her body.  Since Hannibal and Starling meet only at the end of the film, director Ridley Scott uses this technique as a substitute for Hannibal's amorous intentions. It's no surprise Foster turned the role down.<br /><br />The last third of Hannibal features Starling, the ridiculously corrupt Justice Department officer Paul Krendler (Ray Liotta, who looks the part), and everybody's favorite cannibal at a dinner for two.  The dinner scene only manages to be disgusting because there's not a shred of sympathy for Krendler, unlike Pazzi who had a gorgeous wife and so much to lose.  By the conclusion Hannibal is in full slasher mode, complete with the ability to silence dogs at a glance, terrorize gun-wielding victims into inaction with just a knife, and otherwise pop-up and disappear as needed for him to abduct his victims.  In short, Hannibal goes from being a three-dimensional character to a one-dimensional killer.<br /><br />Conversely, Starling is a straight arrow.  So straight, in fact, that it becomes inconceivable that there's any room for romance with Hannibal.  She is polite, but frigid - her lack of connection with any other character leaves her as a cipher with about as much charm as a wax statue.  The final showdown between Starling and Hannibal, which is considerably different from the book, detracts from all the romantic opera chatter that took place in the first third of the film.  Hannibal has difficulty staying true to its convictions.<br /><br />But if you know anything about the original source material, you can understand why.  Hannibal, the book, is a grand guignol of ridiculous characters and over-the-top violence.  Hannibal, the movie, is tame in comparison, but not daring enough to conclude the cannibal's movie legacy.g enough to conclude the cannibal's movie legacy.

B0054NQZ9S|5.0|I think this story delivers an important message in a way that is easily relatable for many people. It's a modern-day take on the age old \\"rags-to-riches-to-rags\\" theme. The main characters, who have worked their way up the corporate ladder to a life of luxury that others only dream of, are suddenly faced with the cold, hard reality that it can all be taken away in no time at all, no matter who you are. What a crushing blow to learn that years of time and dedication are no longer of value thanks to the almighty to the bottom line. Greed is a powerful force that thrives on gaining more with no regard for the destruction left in its wake.     One of the most significant events during my childhood was that of my father losing his job in 1970 and the impact it had on our lives. It was a major blow to his self esteem. He worked for the same mega-corporation that both of my grandfathers had retired from after decades of hard work. He had planned to do the same. He was part of a generation that grew up believing in the benefits of dedicating yourself to a good company and therefore being respected and rewarded for your effort.     He was blindsided by a massive downsizing layoff and was distraught at suddenly being unable to support our single-income family. Jobs were scarce because so many other men were in the same situation, and ultimately my parents were forced to sell our newly built home - the dream house they had worked and saved for over the years. They bought a mobile home and we lived on the corner of a relatives property. He found a job at a small manufacturing plant where he was miserable but nonetheless busted his back for 30+ years ... and then was ultimately screwed out of his promised retirement package when the company later went under.     I think it's because of that I realized early in my career that business is a game involving players who are driven by their own particular motivation. Some people are team players whose goal is working for the greatergood of everyone involved, while others have no true concern for anyone but themselves and will step on everyone who gets in their way. You've got to play the game to succeed, but I believe it's the honest, empathetic and hard-working people who are the winners no matter how many dollar signs are on their paycheck.

0307716511|4.0|If you are already reading or plan to read Sister: A Novel by Rosamund Lupton, do not read this post.  This review contains spoilers, and I refuse to be the spoiler of a great book.    Because that's what this book was; it was great.  I loved it.  I appreciated the writing more than the story itself, but the plot did keep me hooked until the end.  I'm not typically a fan of epistolary novels (\\"epistolary\\" = word of the day); I find them choppy and difficult to read, but I felt that Lupton did it right.  Despite Beatrice's first-person, past-tense account directed at her sister, I found myself forgetting that it was a story told through letters, and I think I enjoyed this book so much specifically because it was written in that style.  It was a smooth but insightful read.  And it was the author's first novel, which I didn't find out until after I read the book. I found that impressive, considering that it was exceedingly well-written and interesting.    What I wasn't crazy about: the ending.  And when I say the ending, I mean thevery ending, because the few pages right before the end were juicily shocking, in my opinion.  We discover the identiy of Tess' killer, but the real blow is learning how much of the tale Beatrice fabricated in order to elicit comfort and have an outlet through which to tell her story.  Mr. Wright, the lawyer that Beatrice uses as a vehicle through which to narrate the account as she gives a witness statement was fabricated, along with other crucial parts of the narrative.  In the final pages, we also find out that Beatrice is about to suffer the same gruesome fate as Tess (murdered in a public restroom, alone, dazed and frightened), and were the author to leave it that way, I think it would have been even more chilling and a hell of a lot more surprising.    Yes, I'm suggesting that the author should have killed off the main character-- a main character that I actually liked.  At the very end, though, at the 11th hour, with death knocking atthe door and Beatrice ready to surrender to it, we learn that she is about to be rescued.  Call me crazy, but I found this deflating.  Should you read it? Definitely.

030745455X|4.0|The enthusiasm of this review is tempered by the crushing disappointment of the third installment of the series.  But taken alone, The Girl Who Played with Fire is brilliant. I scanned with curiosity and amusement some of the one-star reviews, and found the criticism unwarranted. I actually enjoyed the detailed descriptions of the purchases for Salander's apartment; call me strange. The everyday details of living made the story more real to me. They're in Sweden, where are they supposed to shop except IKEA? The only details I remember given about a computer were when Salander wrecked her laptop and had to buy a new one. What is Larsson supposed to say? \\"She populated her home with assorted products purchased at a self-assembly home furnishings retail chain and had some generic frozen pizza for dinner... \\"  I neither know nor care whether Larsson had product placement deals with these companies. We live in a consumerist society, deal with it. Those are the least of his literary offenses. Yes, the premise is farfetched. I'm skeptical that in \\"real life\\" Salander could pull off the massive embezzlement she did, but if suspense thrillers had to be like reality no one would ever have heard of Ian Fleming. All that matters is that there's a compelling story that's quick-paced and entertaining to read, and TGWPWF delivers. If the haters hadn't stopped reading in a fit of pique when they got to the IKEA stuff, they might have found an enjoyable read.<br /><br />That said, TGWPWF brings some things from the first novel into clearer focus.  Where the first and third novels suffer under the weight of excessive exposition and dialogue, this one is more action driven.  I couldn't put it down. Dragon Tattoo was all about Blomkvist; Salander is the star here, less one-dimensional and more sympathetic. The sinister Advokat Bjurman resurfaces to chilling effect. I could have done without the seemingly pointless subplot early in the book about an American couple which played no role in the rest of the story. Needless side characters and subplots are Larsson's most annoying literary quirk.  It's just a shame he didn't wrap up the series with a concluding novel worthy of this gem, because TGWPWF leaves you craving more. Make like a Larsson character and put on another pot of coffee, because you'll stay up late reading this one.rest of the story. B0036S4C6G|4.0|At this point, the Goon Squad has reached the highest possible status for a best seller (including the Pulitzer) and I was curious to read it. I would definitely advise you to download the book: it is superbly written and a most unusual novel - more a collection of inter-connected short stories than a traditional novel.<br /><br />Egan's virtuosity is impressive. She manages to:<br />1. weave the plot from diverse characters all linked together by their past experiences in punk rock music; as a result, we are given a vast and varying vision of a particular world of musicians and their fans, spanning three decades, and even ending sometime in the (near) future: an ambitious goal, and one that is substantially achieved (I have never learned so much about the punk rock scene);<br />2. write from vastly diverse points of views: each time, a different voice matches the different point of view of a different character, and nowhere is this more evident than in the chapter where Rob, the half gay friend of one of the novel's main characters, a mixed-up woman named Sasha, goes swimming in the East River and drowns.<br /><br />This particular chapter, written from the \\"you\\" standpoint is a genuine literary tour de force. Indeed \\"you\\" is a  rare and notoriously hard to manage point of view (most books are written either in the first or third person). Yet the author manages to describe everything that is going on between no less than three characters and also, by adopting the \\"you\\" mode, she succeeds in expressing in an original way the fact that Rob (the one who uses \\"you\\" in the chapter) suffers from identity problems (he's gay, but not quite, he's in love with Sasha but not quite etc.)<br /><br />But there are some drawbacks. For example, one of the last chapters, meant to describe how Sasha eventually finds her own equilibrium in marriage and motherhood, is laid out like a series of scientific diagrams, graphs and boxes of varying shape, that while intriguing (the graphs are imaginatively used), can be irritating. Apart from the fact that it was hard to read on the Kindle(the font size was too small and couldn't be enlarged), it left too much out of what you expect in a work of fiction, in particular with regard to plot development and linkages between characters, events and setting. It was more like a scientific summary than a novel. If intended as an experimental form of fiction, it didn't really work: it left you felt you, the reader, out in the cold, yearning for more. In no way could you connect and enter in the mind of any of the characters ensconced in a diagram. No empathy was possible.<br /><br />Still, in spite of the drawbacks, the book is so innovative and so well written, that I highly recommend it.irritating. Apart from the fact that it was hard to read on the Kindle(the font size was too small and couldn't be enlarged), it left too much out of what you expect in a work of fiction, in particular with regard to plot development and linkages between characters, events and setting. Still, in spite of the drawbacks, the book is so innovative and so well written, that I highly recommend it.

Three words into her description (\\"It's about...\\") I said, \\"You know what? It sounds gruesome and it is, fortunately Collins understands this and doesn't get into the gory details save but a few times when it's necessary.<br /><br />Woven into this dramatic and unnerving theme, is a love story which may sound sappy and unnecessary but it truly adds another dimension to the dynamic relationships between the characters.  Additionally it allows for a few very unexpected, but welcome, surprises.<br /><br />The range of emotions I ran through while reading The Hunger Games was intense.  I'm not an emotional person per se but to find myself feeling dejected, elated, saddened, relieved, enchanted, impassioned, excited all in the first few chapters, I was astounded.  The book is a rollercoaster of a ride, and it doesn't just stop after the first few pages. Absolutely, yes.  And I've purposefully refrained from giving details but rather tried to give the emotional feel of the book. JUDGMENT CALL:    It's a shame that \\"Stargate Universe\\" was not given the chance to make their corrections and a shame that many fans abandoned the series.    I've seen so many sci-fi TV series that started out dismally, finding new synergy and writers to improve the storyline of a series and I've seen networks give these sci-fi shows a chance.  In some ways, I am quite surprised that SyFy abandoned \\"Stargate Universe\\" since the network have been active in promoting the series for nearly two decades.  But a lot of networks known for a certain niche are starting to incorporate different programming and in the case of SyFy, Fridays which were typically Stargate night has been replaced by WWE Smackdown.    Granted, it's all business and the fact was that \\"Stargate Universe\\" lost a lot of its viewers after the first premiere episode didn't help and to make things worse, episode after episode,  none were willing to come back and give it another chance for the second season.  It's unfortunate because the writers did make the adjustment and improved upon the first season.    In fact, the talent seemed to gel, you can feel that everyone is much more comfortable playing the character, especially seeing the characters put into a variety of intense situations.  The character development of this series was much better this time around.  I bring up \\"Star Trek: The Next Generation\\" as an example because the first season didn't do all that great and the characters weren't yet as comfortable until that second season and we see it with SG-U, the only difference is that ST:TNG was given a chance and it was also syndicated.    It's sad because sci-fi TV series seem to have taken a back seat to vampire series and in the past, we would possibly have seen a network give SG-U a chance to prove itself but in this case, times have changed, business haschanged and even the SyFy Channel has changed.    So, the question is...who is this DVD for?    For one, the fans of the series will surely pick it up as this is one jam-packed DVD with plenty of special features and audio commentary.  And I can only hope that those who were watching the first season, will pick this second and final season on DVD to see how this season did improve.    But the fact is that the series ends with a cliffhanger and there is no sight of any conclusion coming any time soon.  But even as a cliffhanger, writing-wise, it was touching of how they utilized Eli's character and sets up things for hopefully something in the future.  May it be in  by novel, movie or something.  And I hope there is some closure for fans...hopefully something rather than nothing.    I was hoping SyFy would have allowed the series to tie up some loose ends with a movie, afterall, \\"Stargate\\" has been part of SyFy for a nearly two decades, at least give those fans who did stick with the series some closure.  But I understand, at the end of the day, it's all business, afterall...    Overall, \\"Stargate Universe: The Complete Final Season\\" was a much better season than the first and it was getting good.  The DVD is a wonderful set and a great sendoff for those who did take their time and stayed with the series from beginning through end and I also do recommend this series for those who stopped at the first season, I'm quite sure they will enjoy this second, final season much more, especially knowing that there is so much included on this DVD set.  Once again, fans will definitely want to pick this DVD Set up!    Despite what the critics and hardcore fans of the franchise may have felt towards \\"SG-U\\", I give credit to the writers for making the much needed adjustments and making this final season worth watching and a final season DVD worth owning.

0811870413|3.0|I've just begun to use this book, so this is a preliminary opinion but--it's surprisingly badly edited, in that the instructions are so vague. To mix a starter, e.g., we're told to fill \\"a small container\\" halfway with water. Please, why on earth not give a measurement: use a standard-measure cup of water? a standard-measure half-cup of water? a standard-measure 1 1/2 cups of water? Containers can range a fair amount in size and still be small. We're told to add \\"a handful of flour\\" to make a \\"thick batter.\\" If a handful of flour produces a thin batter, have we used too much water? Should we start again with less? Or should we just keep adding flour? If we add three or four handfuls of flour and the batter is thicker but it's still questionable whether to call it \\"thick,\\" have we probably added enough, or did we really start with too much water? If the latter, is it ok to keep going with more flour, would that just mean we'd end up with a lot of starter, or should we probably start again with less water? Then \\"place in a cool, shaded spot for 2 or 3 days.\\" In much of the U.S.--unless you run a/c 24/7, even when you're at work--the only cool spot in summer is your refrigerator. But--is that cool? Or is that cold--cold enough to kill a starter? Between your kitchen closet and your refrigerator, which is the better bet--or should you just forget about breadmaking in summer and wait until fall? Photos alongside the text I think are meant to help guide us, but they're decoratively blurred, printed in a faded, bleached b&w--not clear enough to be completely clarifying, particularly with scale. More important, there are no captions. Which means there's no sense of time: is this photo how the starter's meant to look when you first mix it? Or after that 2 or 3 days?     And this is the first page of the first recipe.    It's a very handsome book--and the bread looks wonderful--but it's one of those books where more thought has gone into the *style* of the writing, and into the design, than into how best to communicate to the reader. The imagined audience seems to live in the cool climate of San Francisco or rural Marin County; there's no attempt whatsoever to imagine the range of circumstances in which a reader might live. Incidentally, I've made bread many times before, I'm not coming to it for the first time; I just think a book like this should be clearly written.

B004WJRF6C|4.0|When Paul McCartney released his first solo album and the first true solo studio album by a Beatle in 1970, it was almost universally panned. While it certainly doesn't matchup with any of the Beatles albums, it has aged better than a lot of the music of the time. Mr. McCartney recorded the album at his farm and its greatest asset is the simple, down-home feel that permeates it. You can almost picture the newlywed and new father just tooling around the house and living the country life and whenever the mood struck him, laying down a new track. He plays all the instruments and other than some backing vocals by Linda, sings all the songs. There are several instrumentals on the album and a couple of songs like \\"Man We Was Lonely\\" that are basically just a couple of lines sung over and over, but \\"Maybe I'm Amazed\\" is a true gem. The song ranks up there with anything he's done in his solo career and a wouldn't be slighted by songs in the Beatles catalog. Other standout tracks include the sweet \\"Every Night\\", \\"Junk\\" & \\"That Would Be Something\\" & \\"Teddy Boy\\". There are a few too many instrumentals like \\"Hot As Sun/Glasses\\", the bizarre, jungle like \\"Kreen-Akrore\\" and an unneeded instrumental version of \\"Junk\\".  Despite the wish for more fully composed songs, the half-completed tracks and instrumentals add to the album's rustic charm.  Of the three outtakes from the original album sessions, none of them really add much with the best of three ironically being an instrumental \\"Don't Cry Baby\\".

B0002ZYE1Y|5.0|-Some might say I'm giving Nightwish too much credit. In this style of metal, genuinly good lyrics are somewhat difficult to come by. Nightwish comes down like a breath of fresh air with their poetic, but not overly pretintious lyrics, which I really appriciate about this band. Then there's the legendary Tarja. It will come as no suprise to fans that her vocal duties on this album are amazing. She simpily washes the floor with 90% of the compitition in female fronted metal, leaving her as one of the undisputed godesses of this genre. She has the ability to effortlessly hit notes that would split the necks of the majority of 'soprano singers' out there, but not only is her technique worthy of worship, but the way in which she lays out the melodys that thoumas writes is truely a marvel. The emotional convection of Tarja's voice takes the listener on a magical journey that can really only be described as... Nightwish. It is a spiritual journey, exploring the emotions of the soul. Tragedy and sadness, yet with a constant shining ray of hope ever present on this record. And of course, you can't forget about Marco, who took the role as co-vocalist on their previous release 'Century Child.' He quickly made a name for himself as a singer more than capable of complimenting Tarja with his instantly recognizable vocals. Intense and filled with rage, he sores high above the moon with his chilling voice, that contrasts greatly with tarja's equally chilling but less angry vocals. It is my belief that Marco is the best thing that ever happened to Nightwish, and is perhaps the greatest male singer in Finland. Unfortanetly it took me a little while to hop on board, seeing as Marco's vocals is somthing that you either love or hate. Needless to say at this point, I'm a HUGE fan of now, so that it has become difficult for me to decide whom I prefer between the two singers. I'll just leave it at this, they both rule.   Emmpu Vourian is in my opinion, one of the most underrated guitar players in metal, primarily due to the fact that he doesent shred at lightning speed. That just isn't his style, and thank god for that, because it really wouldn't work for Nightwish's sound. Whether or not he is genuinly capable of shredding is irrevelent. His heavy riffage, and magical yet tastefull soloing is one of the primary sources of the Nightwish formula. Don't overlook his subtle touch.  Next is Jukka. His drumming is simple, but heartfelt, and he creates the heartbeat of the band in a way that few drummers can. His performance here is perhaps his best.  Now, the reason I say that this is amoung the most influencial albums in metal, is for the simple reason that these is the blueprints of modern symphonic power metal. Granted, Nightwish is not the first to have done this, but they are the best ever to do it, and there is no other band that has captured the world beneath the spell that has been cast by Nightwish. This is a spell that has created a legion of immatators. Female fronted metal can be summed up in four names. The Gathering, After Forever, Epica and Nightwish. All else in female fronted power/symphonic/gothic metal bows before them. They are the big four of these subgenres.  Notable tracks:  Ghost Love Score  The Siren  Planet hell  Higher than Hope  Nemo  Dark Chest of Wonders  This is my somewhat less than humble opinion of a band that I owe my Alligance. Buy it if you have ever cared for symphonic metal.
